Republic of the Philippines 
MINDANAO STATE UNIVERSITY-ILIGAN STATE UNIVERSITY 
Iligan City 
 
 
Program:  Master of Science in Computer Applications 
Proponent: Department of Computer Applications, College of Computer Studies 
  MSU-Iligan Institute of Technology 
Summary of Changes:  
 
1. The Master of Science in Computer Applications approved by the MSU Board of Regent 
under Resolution No.7, Series of 2004 is enhanced to conform to CHED Memo. No. 15 
Series of 2019 aligned to Master of Science (MSc) Academic Track   
 
2. The number of units of the revised Master of Science in Computer Applications is changed 
from 37 units to 31 units to conform to CHED Memo. No. 15 Series of 2019. 
 
3. The proposed curriculum has  
 
12 units of the following core courses:  
Advanced Computer Organization, Advanced Operating System, Research 
Design and Methodology & System Development with Emerging 
Technologies;  
9 units of Specialization Courses;  
1 unit of ICT for Peace and Development; 
3 units of Systematic Review and Research Seminar in ICT; and  
6 units of Thesis. 
 
Total of 31 units 
 
4. The proposed curriculum requires students to pass the Comprehensive Examination and 
publish at least one (1) article in a refereed journal or juried creative work outlet.

NATURE OF THE PROGRAM BEING REVISED 
 
 
Program: Master of Science in Computer Applications 
Proponent: Department of Computer Applications, College of Computer Studies 
  MSU–Iligan Institute of Technology 
Features of the Proposed Revision: 
1. The Master of Science in Computer Applications approved by the MSU Board of Regent 
under Resolution No.7, Series of 2004 is enhanced to conform to CHED Memo. No. 15 
Series of 2019 aligned to Master of Science (MSc) Academic Track   
 
2. The number of units of the revised Master of Science in Computer Applications is changed 
from 37 units to 31 units  to conform to CHED Memo. No. 15 Series of 20 19. For those 
who will be part of the ERDT Scholarship they will be having 34 units, and for bridging 
students they will be take 40 units. 
 
3. The proposed curriculum has  
 
12 units of the following core courses:  
Advanced Computer Organization, Advanced Operating System, Methods 
of Research & System Development with Emerging Technologies;  
9 units of Specialization Courses;  
1 unit of ICT for Peace and Development; 
3 units of Systematic Review and Research Seminar in ICT; and  
6 units of Master’s Thesis. 
 
Total of 31 units 
 
4. The proposed curriculum requires students to pass the Comprehensive Examination and 
publish at least one (1) article in a refereed journal or juried creative work outlet.

FORM 04 
 
RATIONALE, OBJECTIVES, STRATEGIES AND PROCESSES 
 
College: College of Computer Studies 
Department: Computer Applications 
Branch/Unit: MSU-Iligan Institute of Technology 
 
Proposal: Revision of the Program Master of Science in Computer Applications (MSCA) 
                                                                                
1. Rationale 
 
The 21 st century has witnessed the rapid advancements of technology, particularly in the field of 
emerging technologies, including bioinformatics, medical informatics and services, military 
advancement applications and ICT for sustainability. These technologies have revolutionized our way of 
life such that societies have felt the advent of Industry 4.0, the fourth industrial revolution, through the 
highly autonomous and self -configuring sensor -based production systems. With Industry 4.0, comes 
connected technology such as Internet of Things (IoT), machine learning, artificial intelligence, and big 
data analytics. All these increasing demands from various technological processes and advancements 
need 21st century skills and knowledge. 
 
Therefore, in the academe, vari ous programs will have to be strategized such that students’ skills to be 
acquired will match the demands of various organizations, industries, academic institutions and societies 
vis-a-vis current and future technological advancements. Academic programs s hould increase or 
maximize students’ learning potentials and empower teachers to be more engaged in some advanced 
industry-leading processes and developments. 
Anent to this, the Department of Computer Applications takes further steps in re-aligning its curriculum 
with the current demands in the local and the global arena through the proposed revisions of the graduate 
program Master of Science in Computer Applications. The MSCA, approved by the MSU Board of 
Regent under Resolution No.7, Series of 2004, aimed to keep abreast with the changes instituted by the 
Commission on Higher Education under the CHED Memorandum Order No.15, Series of 2019, stating 
the POLICIES, STANDARDS, AND GUIDELINES FOR GRADUATE PROGRAMS and the 
PHILIPPINE QUALIFICATIONS FRAMEWORK (PQF), a national policy that describes the levels of 
educational qualifications and sets the standards for qualification outcomes. Hence, the MSCA shall cater 
to graduates of the undergraduate program Bachelor of Science in Computer Applications who are 
seeking to further their knowledge and skills to match local and international industry needs.  
Based on the Computing Curricula Series 2020 presented Association for Computing Machinery (ACM) 
and IEEE Computer Society (IEEE -CS) last December 31, 2020 - covering undergraduate degree 
programs in Computer Engineering, Computer Science, Information Systems, Information Technology, 
and Software Engineering, the commonalities and differences among computing disciplines are 
illustrated in figure 1 the Graphical Views o f the Computing Disciplines below. Focuses from 1 -level 
Computer Hardware and Architecture, 2-level System Infrastructure, 3-level Software Development, 4-
level Application technologies and 5-level Organizational System Issues, through its development level 
of assessment with theoretical (Theory, Principles & Innovation) and applied (Application, Deployment 
& Configuration)

Moreover, the proposed MSCA revision shall, in the future, allow students coming from different 
disciplines, in both undergraduate and graduate programs, to be admitted into the program. The students 
will undergo bridging courses to be aligned with the MSCA specialization. The proposed revised MSCA 
curriculum shall equip our graduate students with the analytical and technical skills they will need for 
the Industry 4.0 Implementation. As students gain further knowledge on computer applications, they will 
also develop research and communication skills; learn to evaluate their work and gain adequate 
preparation for the workplace or for further advancement through allied doctorate degree programs.

2. Proposed Program Outcomes, Performance Indicators, and Assessment 
With the Outcomes-Based Education (OBE) framework, the expected outcomes, performance 
indicators, and assessment upon completion of the program are listed below. 
2.1 Program Outcomes for Graduate Programs 
The Master of Science in Computer Applications adopts the Philippine Qualifications Framework (PQF) 
Level 7 which has an expected learning outcomes along the three domains: knowledge, skills and values; 
application; and degree of independence. 
Domain Descriptor 
Knowledge Skills 
and Attitudes (D1) 
Demonstrate advanced knowledge and skills in Computer Applications and 
organization for professional practice, self -directed research and/or lifelong 
learning. 
Application (D2) Apply knowledge and skills in Computer Applications for professional practice, 
self-directed research and/or lifelong learning. 
Degree of 
Independence (D3) 
Practice a high substantial degree of confidence that involves work which 
exercises leadership with ethical responsibility, to organize teams for multi-
disciplinary and interdisciplinary fields of specialization, providing solutions to 
society for various technological problems in the national and international 
level. 
 
Program Outcomes Common to All CCS Graduate Programs 
Ability to use advanced knowledge and skills in ICT to promote peace and development.

3. Strategies and Processes Involved in the Revision 
3.1 The Institute summits held on two separate occasions probed into the capacity of the department 
in the next 5 to 10 years. It was strongly recommended that the Department revises its graduate 
program so that these will be vertically aligned, in terms of pr ogram outcomes, with its 
undergraduate program.  
 
3.2 The Department of Computer Applications has reviewed the existing curricula in view of the 
proposed CHED Memorandum Order No.15 Series 2019 entitled “SUBJECT: POLICIES, 
STANDARDS, AND GUIDELINES FOR GRADUATE PROGRAMS.  
 
3.3 The Department also examined the related national standard set by CHED Memorandum Order 
No.7 Series 2010 entitled “REVISE POLICIES, STANDARDS, AND GUIDELINES FOR 
GRADUATE PROGRAM IN INFORMATION TECHNOLOGY EDUCATION (ITE)”  and the 
“MANUAL FOR CURRICULUM REVISIONS AND DEVELOPMENT AND OFFERING OF 
NEW CURRICULAR PROGRAMS” of MSU System. 
 
3.4 The Department was also mandated to work for its CHED Center of Development or Excellence 
accreditation by the Institute’s higher administration and thus, it needs to build its faculty force, 
productivity and academic scholarship in order to achieve this goal.  
 
3.5 The Department also reviewed the existing curricula due to its application for AACCUP 
accreditation and Center of Development (COD) - CHED. And a serious e valuation of the 
present program in terms of its relevance to the changing landscape of the educational system.  
 
3.6 Lastly an informal consultation and feedback from some graduates now working in the 
Philippines and abroad were solicited. 
 
3.7 The department of Computer Applications referred the changes to the approved graduate 
handbook BOR Resolution No. 454, series of 2022.  
 
4. Implementation Scheme  
 
A.) To apply for admission into the program, a prospective student must have: 
1. A baccalaureate degree in Computer Science, Information Technology, Information 
Management, Engineering, Mathematics or other fields that may prepare them for the 
specialization they wants to pursue; 
 
2. Demonstrable proficiency in at least one high -level programming language which may be 
through passing a formal course in programming or a Programming Proficiency Examination 
or an equivalent certification examination; 
 
3. For other fields (not aligned to CCS), students are required to take bridging courses: 
 
a) BCA major subjects 
Fundamentals of Computer Applications 2 (BCA 121) or its equivalent. 
Computer Programming (CCC 101) or its equivalent.

4.1 Admission Procedure 
The student is required to prepare the following documents/items: 
1. Two (2) sets photocopy for the Office of the CCS Dean through the College Graduate 
Coordinator and one (1) set certified true copies of Transcript of Records for the Registrar’s 
Office; 
2. Two (2) pcs. 2×2 ID pasted to application forms for the Office of the CCS Dean through the 
College Graduate Coordinator and another two (2) pcs. With name at the back for the 
Registrar’s Office;

3. Two (2) photocopy copies of Transfer Credentials/Honorable Dismissal for the Office of the

Office (required only to non -MSU-IIT alumnus). Present a promissory note that the same be 
submitted within a month after regular enrolment, if yet to be secured;

sought (or promissory note if yet to be secured); see Guidelines on Forms, re: 
“Recommendation Form for Admission to a Graduate Program”;

from the program adviser/coordinator); 
6. Photocopy of marriage certificate (for married women) for the Registrar’s Office. 
Once the above documents/items are prepared, the student should accomplish the following:  
1. Fill up 2 sets of “Application for Admission to a Graduate Program” forms and submit these, 
together with items 1-5 above, to the Program Adviser/Department Coordinator stationed at the 
Department. 
2. Department Program Adviser/Coordinator evaluates application and interviews applicant (an 
admission exam with appropriate fees may be administered in some programs);  
3. Department Program Adviser/Coordinator signs the forms and endorses the 2 sets of the 
application documents to the College Graduate Coordinator; 
4. The CCS Dean signs the two sets of application forms and issues Notice of Admission. Copies 
of the Notice of Admission are attached to the application documents. 
5. One set of the application documents is filed at the Office of the CCS Dean. The other set is 
returned to the Program Adviser/Coordinator for filing in the Department.  
6. Students get a copy of the Notice of Admission and proceed to Program Adviser/Coordinator 
for enrollment.  
 
4.2 Classification of Students  
Students upon admission shall be classified as follows: 
1. Regular - a full -fledged regular graduate student who satisfactorily complies with all the 
requirements for admission, including those prescribed by the department/college offering the 
degree. 
 
2. Conditional - an applicant with deficiencies, e.g., prerequisite courses, but satisfies all the basic 
requirements for admission. The said deficiencies should be satisfied on or before the end of 
the second semester of the first year of attendance. A student on conditional status should be 
dismissed from the program if they fails to earn a GPA of 2.00 or better in the first semester 
of residence for the master’s degree, or a GPA of 1.75 or better for the doctoral degree.  
 
3. Probational - an applicant who fails to earn an undergraduate GPA of 2.00 or better but has 
proven to be capable to pursue a master’s degree. A student on probation should be dismissed 
from the program if they fails to earn a GPA of 2.00 or better in the first semester of residence 
in the master’s degree. 
  
4. Special - an applicant who, in writing, does not intend to pursue a graduate degree but seeks 
admission to the Office of Graduate Studies to register in some courses.

4.3 Requirements for Completing the MSCA 
To qualify for the MSCA degree, the following conditions need to be satisfied: 
1. Completion of all coursework indicated in the MSCA curriculum; 
 
2. Passing the comprehensive exam;  
3. A general cumulative Grade Point Average of at least 2.0; repetition of courses can be done to 
be able to meet this cumulative GPA requirement. The higher grade for the repeated courses 
shall be the ones considered in the computation of the GPA.  
4. Completion and successful presentation of a Graduate Thesis. The solution envision in the 
Thesis must have been implemented through the development of a working prototype. 
Submission of bound copies of the Thesis is required.  
5. Evidence of acceptance of an article for publication to a refereed journal. 
 
Grading System 
In accordance with the MSUS grading system, grades are given as follows: 
1.00 - Excellent 2.50 - Satisfactory IP - In Progress 
1.25 - Excellent 2.75   - Satisfactory P - Passing 
1.50 - Very Good 3.00 - Passing F - Failure 
1.75 - Very Good 5.00 - Failure    
2.00 - Good INC   - Incomplete    
2.25 - Good           DRP - Dropped    
 
Residence Requirement and Time Limit for Graduation: 
All the requirements for the MSCA degree shall be completed in the required residency 
period as indicated in the BOR-approved Graduate School Philosophical Framework. 
Note: The Graduate School general policies for admission, retention and completion of a 
degree shall be adhered to by the Master of Science in Computer Applications 
program. 
  
4.4 Important Curricular Policies to Maintain Quality as Required by OBE and PSG 
 
In order to maintain quality of the Master of Science in Computer Applications program, the College 
of Computer Studies which manages the program shall strictly adhere and comply to the policies and 
mandate of the Institute, the MS US, and the CHED memorandum circulars for outcomes -based 
education and policies for graduate level education, apart from the standards set by the following: 
• CMO 15, s. 2019 (Policies, Standards and Guidelines for Graduate Programs) 
• CMO 46, s. 2012 (Policy Standard to Enhance Quality Assurance (QA) in Philippine 
higher Education through Outcomes-Based and Typology-Based QA  
• MSU-IIT Vision, Mission and Goals

5. Curriculum Description  
The multi-disciplinary program, Master of Science in Computer Applications (MSCA), is geared 
to satisfy the demand for ICT practitioners in various fields. It is flexible and can be tailored to the 
growing needs of industry, business, and academe in the country, especially in the Southern 
Philippines.  
The course delivery of classes is as combination of synchronous and asynchronous learning called 
hybrid or blended learning  of which combines traditional face -to-face classroom instruction with 
online learning. While face-to-face and hybrid are being implemented the full implementation of 
pure online classes and arranged classes for future reference will abide with the MSU System Policy. 
The program was introduced in 1997 in answer to the requirements outlined in the CHED 
Mindanao Advanced Education Program. While graduates of the program have mostly served in the 
academe, they were primarily empowered to organize information in the solution of problems in 
science, engineering, and business.  
The program is designed to be dynamic and flexible; it can accommodate interdisciplinary and 
multidisciplinary programs such as bioinformatics, social computing, environmental computing and 
other hardware related courses. It provides the demand of research ers, innovators and developers 
for Industry 4.0.

CURRICULUM MAP 
 
 
Program Outcomes Specific to Master of Science in Computer Applications 
 
1. Demonstrate mastery of advanced knowledge in the field of computer applications by 
solving complex computing problems and tasks, and apply relevant approaches, 
resources, and emerging computing technologies. 
2. Relate and apply practical skills (life-long learning skills), ideas, and associated 
technologies to novel and emerging problems and societal issues in relation to Computer 
Applications 
3. Conduct research and foster collaboration, and effectively share the result in community 
and conferences presentations in both written and oral format 
 
The curriculum map provided, layouts the courses as rows and program outcomes specific to 
MSCA as columns. The map indicates the extent to which the courses in the curriculum correspond 
to the program outcomes. The following are the d escriptions used to correlate the outcomes and 
the courses: 
 
*L: facilitates learning of competency 
  P: allows students to practice competency (no input, but competency is evaluated) 
  O: opportunity for development (no input or evaluation but competency is practiced)

FORM 10 
 
COURSE DESCRIPTIONS 
 
MASTER OF SCIENCE IN COMPUTER APPLICATIONS 
 
 
MCA201  ADVANCED COMPUTER ORGANIZATION 
 Basic computer logic: truth tables; logic equations, gates, combinatorial logic; 
basic computer arithmetic; binary numbers; addition and subtraction; floating point 
representation; system hierarchy; integrated circuit technology; performance; 
metrics; closing benchmarks; Amhdal’s law; instruction sets and operations; MIPS; 
assembly Language; machine language; examples of other instruction sets. 
 Credit:    3 units (3 hrs lec, 0 hrs lab) 
 Prerequisite(s):    None 
 
 
 
MCA202  ADVANCED OPERATING SYSTEMS  
  The study of a broad range of behaviors arising from complex system design, 
including low-level operating systems, resource sharing and scheduling, interactions 
between hardware and software, network -protocol design and implementation, 
separation of mutually distrusting parties on a common platform, and control of 
distributed-system behaviors such as concurrency and data replication. 
 Credit:    3 units (3 hrs lec, 0 hrs lab) 
 Prerequisite(s):    None 
 
 
 
MCA203  SYSTEM DEVELOPMENT WITH EMERGING TECHNOLOGIES 
 This course focuses on the development of software application using the web, 
mobile and emerging technologies. Emphasis is on the requirements management, 
interface design, usability, and deployment including ethical and legal considerations. 
Topics include file -handling, front -end development with create -retrieve-update-
delete (CRUD) modules, event-driven programming, and data manipulation. 
  
Credit:    
 
3 units (3 hrs lec, 0 hrs lab) 
 Prerequisite(s):    None

MINDANAO STATE UNIVERSITY

A. Bonifacio Avenue, Tibanga, 9200 Iligan City         
Website: http://www.msuiit.edu.ph 
Telephone Nos: (063)221-4050 to 55 Local  4165, 
Tele/fax(063) 223-3794,  e-mail:registrar@g.msuiit.edu.ph 
MCA221  ADVANCED EMBEDDED SYSTEMS  
 This course will introduce advanced topics in the principles of embedded 
system, measurement system design in Analog to Digital Converters and Sensors 
used in interfacing, ARM processor fundamentals, design of FPGA -based 
embedded systems using VHDL Fundamentals subprogram and files - including 
data stream management, embedded systems for multi -media, real-time embedded 
systems, and embedded system security. 
 Credit:    3 units (3 hrs lec, 0 hrs lab) 
 Prerequisite(s):    None 
 
 
 
MCA222  ADVANCED DIGITAL SIGNAL PROCESSING 
 This course will introduce students to explore sampled signals and digital 
processing, random signals, representing signals and systems, temporal and spatial 
signal processing, frequency analysis of signals and discrete-time filters and recursive 
filters. A conversion of the real-world signals to digital form and then conversion back 
to the analog form following the processing. These include the conversion from analog 
to digital and vice versa, digital filtering, transforms, noise and its implications, 
estimation and prediction. 
 Credit:    3 units (3 hrs lec, 0 hrs lab) 
 Prerequisite(s):    None 
 
 
 
 
MCA223  ADVANCED COMPUTER VISION 
 This course introduces the fundamentals of computer vision. It will focus on 
fundamental models and algorithms in computer vision, including such topics as 
image formation, image sensing, image filtering, edge extraction, tracking and 
machine learning. The course will also provide an overview of the challenges of 
vision, the common approaches and current techniques. 
 Credit:    3 units (3 hrs lec, 0 hrs lab) 
 Prerequisite(s):    None 
 
 
MCA224  APPLIED MACHINE LEARNING FOR EMBEDDED SYSTEMS  
 This course will introduce students on applying machine learning in 
embedded systems to simplify development of useful machine -learning 
applications, which covers Machine-learning methods, Supervised learning, Neural 
network development, Data preparation, Model development, neural network 
frameworks development methods, discuses platform requirements, and covers 
specialized frameworks and architectures. 
 Credit:    3 units (3 hrs lec, 0 hrs lab) 
 Prerequisite(s):    None

Tele/fax(063) 223-3794,  e-mail:registrar@g.msuiit.edu.ph 
 
MCA225  ADVANCED INTERNET OF THINGS 
 This course focuses on hardware devices in IOT that provides a broad 
introduction to machine learning, datamining, and statistical pattern recognition that 
include: (i) Supervised learning (parametric/non-parametric algorithms, support vector 
machines, kernels, neural networks). (ii) Unsupervised learning (clustering, 
dimensionality reduction, recommender systems, deep learning). (iii) Best practices in 
machine learning (bias/variance theory; innovation process in machine learning and 
AI).  
 Credit:    3 units (3 hrs lec, 0 hrs lab) 
 Prerequisite(s):    None 
 
 
 
MCA226  NETWORK SECURITY FOR IOT 
  
This course introduces the concept of cyber security, its interdisciplinary nature 
and its relation to nation, businesses, society and people. Participating students would 
gain knowledge of various cyber security terminologies, technologies, protocols, 
threat analysis, security principles, security mechanisms, policies, forensics, incidence 
response and methods/practices to secure systems. 
 Credit:    3 units (3 hrs lec, 0 hrs lab) 
 Prerequisite(s):    None 
 
 
MCA227  ADVANCED CLOUD COMPUTING 
 This course provides a theoretical and conceptual knowledge and overview of 
essential to the understanding of cloud computing. It also covers various service level 
security issues and several open challenges for adoption and its future implications. 
Further, this course provides a hands-on, programming-oriented introduction to cloud 
computing with Amazon Web Services (AWS). Students will learn how to use AWS 
services such as S3, EC2, Lambda, and ECS to create apps. 
 Credit:    3 units (3 hrs lec, 0 hrs lab) 
 Prerequisite(s):    None 
 
 
 
MCA228  APPLIED BIG-DATA & ANALYTICS FOR IOT 
 This course focuses on IoT analytics, a strategy  on the industry and specific 
needs of the organization. This includes integration -seamlessly integrate with IoT 
platforms and enterprise stacks, load data, and then merge and manage multiple data 
types, deployment -provide deployment options for a wide ran ge of environments, 
including on -premise installation, cloud hosting, and compatibility with complex 
hybrid ecosystems, customization -let employees easily access and explore data, as

MCA300  MASTER’S THESIS 
  
Finalization of research under the direction of a member of the graduate faculty 
leading to an MS thesis. 
 Credit:    6 units (6 hrs lec, 0 hrs lab) 
 Prerequisite(s):    Must have completed all coursework 
 
 
 
  COMPREHENSIVE EXAMINATION 
 The comprehensive exam provides the opportunity for students to synthesize 
the information that has been presented in academic coursework and be able to apply 
this information in research. The students will apply the skills, knowledge, and 
attitude learned and at the same time the opportunity to analyze pro blems in 
computer application. 
 Credit:    0 units (0 hrs lec, 0 hrs lab) 
 Prerequisite(s):    None 
 
 
 
CCC296 ICT for Peace and Development 
The course is designed to examine the latest ICT technologies and its use and 
implications to individuals, organization, and society. It emphasizes on the value of 
these technologies for the promotion of peace and development given the volatility, 
uncertainty, complexity, and ambiguity of the world. It puts forward the context of 
Mindanao and how it influences or is influenced by the presence and potentials of 
these ICT technologies.  
Credit  : 1 unit (1 hour lecture, 0 hours lab) 
Prerequisite : None 
CCC298 Systematic Review and Research Seminar in ICT 
This course serves a dual role. First and forem ost, A very dynamic and fast 
changing field of study, Information Systems being under the overarching 
computing discipline is constantly faced with updates that may need thorough 
discussion in either depth of breadth. This course, which will discuss special topics 
in Information Systems will cover the latest trends and updates in the Information 
Systems profession that is not covered by the subjects of current curriculum. In the 
midst of rapid global change that poses new opportunities to contribute 
development to different sectors of the society using information systems, this 
course aims to systematically analyze and identify these opportunities. Students in

CCC297  METHODS OF RESEARCH 
 This course guides achieving competence and proficiency in the theory of and 
practice to research. Develop the subject of their research, encourage the formation of 
higher level of trained intellectual ability, critical analysis, rigor, and independence of 
thought, foster individual judgement, and skill in the application of research theory 
and methods, and develop skills required in writing  research proposals, reports, and 
dissertation; 
 Credit:    3 units (3 hrs lec, 0 hrs lab) 
 Prerequisite(s):    None

THE PERFORMANCE INDICATOR FOR EACH OF THE PROGRAM OUTCOMES  
 
Program Outcomes and Indicators 
The following are the Program Outcomes of the Master of Science in Computer Applications  
program.  
 
1. Demonstrate mastery of advanced knowledge in the field of computer applications by 
solving complex computing problems and tasks, and apply relevant approaches, resources, and 
emerging computing technologies. 
 
2. Relate and apply practical skills (life -long learning skills), ideas, and associated 
technologies to novel and emerging problems and societal issues in relation to Computer 
Applications 
 
3. Conduct research and foster collaboration, and effectively share the result in community 
and conferences presentations in both written and oral format 
 
This will be immediately followed by the Graduate Performance Indicators. 
 
A. Critical Thinking and Problem Solving  
- Have developed and demonstrated critical thinking and analytical skills to apply 
computer applications principles to professional practice 
 
Performance Indicator/s: Produced scholarly written works addressing computing 
related issues and its application in organization or society 
 
B. Knowledge and Scholarship  
- Have a capacity to effectively employ Computer Applications related competencies 
in dynamic or unfamiliar contexts 
 
Performance Indicator/s:  
- Documented and demonstrated a case study from a collaborative work on artefacts 
deployment about an organization 
 
C. Communication and Collaboration  
- Show awareness to consolidate and communicate relevant, appropriate and well -
structured information and arguments in academic or professional discourse 
 
Performance Indicator/s:  
- Experience in written and oral examination in technical courses 
- Successfully defended a thesis with a team 
 
D. Ethical Conduct  
- Have an appreciation and high regard for the ITE  professional codes of conduct 
and ethical standards 
 
Performance Indicator/s: 
- Work collaboratively with other experts for a research or project

E. Professional Development  
- Demonstrate a sense of responsibility to foster a high-performance culture 
 
Performance Indicator/s: 
- Presented the thesis in a panel or in academic forum 
- Appreciation of the professional group and ICT related societies

CONTEXT FOR THE CURRICULUM REFORM 
 
The rapid changes in technology and organizational reforms brought about by digitization and the 
advent of artificial intelligence, IOT, and Industry 4.0 in general has a profound impact on technology -
oriented programs of MSU -IIT. The academic land scape of the computing discipline has transcended to 
closely related busin ess area. With national standards/guidelines and recommendations of the 
international organizations in the computer applications discipline and computing in general has affected 
the programs. These ch anges in the international and local context have raised t he need to respond in 
updating the curriculum and its implementation  of the Master of Science in Computer Applications  
program.  
The adoption and usage of ICT in organizations have changed how organizational processes have 
been conducted. The influence of th e computing discipline to the change in organization demands 
Computer Applications discipline to effectively integrate ICT adoption.  In response to the changes at the 
international and national setting, the course syllabi revealed the alignment of the progra m to MSU-IIT’s 
thrusts considering the need of the locale, as well as specific philosophies as articulated in the vision and 
mission of the university and the college. The arti culation of th e common graduate attributes at the 
college and university level s have also shown the realization of the distinct characteristics of an IIT 
graduate.     
The Philippine Qualifications Framework (PQF) vis -à-vis the ASEAN Qualifications Referen ce 
Framework ( AQRF), a s well as the Association of the Computing Machinery has s treamlined the 
competencies with emphasis on outcome -based education. Moreover, the CHED 2019 Memorandum 
Order on Policies, Guidelines, and Standards for Graduate Education has  clearly mentioned the  
requirements of the graduate program and CHED Memorandum Order on ITE graduate program 
indicated requirements for Information Technology Education graduate programs including MS 
Information Systems.  
 The basic tenets observed in the revision of the curriculum are the following: 
 
 
Principles Evidences 
Clarity of focus 
gearing the development of curriculum towards the outcomes expected 
of the students. These should not be short -term targets (weekly, 
semester or yearly) but end results t hat students w ould ach ieve upon 
graduation and beyond

provide learners with various ways to achieve the learning outcomes.

in thei r field of st udy and advanced critical thinking efficiently to

Outcomes, Program Outcomes, Course Outcomes, Unit Outcomes and 
Lesson Outcomes 

--- PAGE 50 ---
44 
 
 
At its most basic level, OBE is where the school and community 
first determine what skills and knowledge students should possess  
at graduation, then work backwards  from there to develop  
curriculum, strategies and materials to help students achieve those 
goals, or exit outcomes’  
(WEAC, 1995 as cited by Donnely, 2007) 
 
In formulating outcomes, our graduate programs are framed along  top to bottom levels of 
outcomes development with the incorporation of long-term PEOs. 
 
 
 
Finally, in order to attain the program outcomes, the following steps are followed: 
 
1. Designing the syllabuses and making sure that assessments achieve the competencies and 
target the course outcomes; 
2. Making sure that course outcomes target program outcomes; 
3. Mapping of the curriculum to determine which course targets the program outcomes; 
4. Rubrics for assessment of each course is discussed and designed among faculty members; 
5. Faculty members teach the course and ensure that students are well oriented what are the  
course outcomes and what competencies they must acquire at the end of the course; (in 
the event that students will have failed to show evidences that they have acquired the 
required competencies, faculty members must discuss with them other assessments) 
6. After the sem ester, faculty members discuss whether the courses will have indeed 
targeted the course outcomes and if they have not, curriculum needs to be remapped; 
7. Faculty members file portfolios of evidences of assessment. 
 
The design of the syllabuses is guided by t he Ou tcomes-Based Education Framework 
(OBE), which emphasizes on two main components in terms of student achievement in an 
academic program. One is the Program Outcomes (POs) which is me asured at the point of 
graduation, and the other, the Prog ram Educational Objectives (PEOs) is assessed over a longer 
period of time (around 4–5 years) after graduation. 

--- PAGE 51 ---
45 
 
OBE is a framework of curriculum design and teaching that focuses on what students can  
actually do after they are taught. OBE addresses the key questions as: 
 
a) What do you want the students to learn?  (Hard Skills) 
b) Why do you want them to learn it?   (Soft skills) 
c) How can you best help students learn it?   (Teaching-Learning Activities) 
d) How will you know what they have learned?  (Assessment Tasks) 
 
The OBE-compliant course syllabus should contain at least the following components: a) 
General course information (title, description, credit units, prerequisite requirements) b) Course  
outcomes and their relationship to the program outcomes c ) Course cov erage that relates the 
course outcomes to topics covered, teaching and learning activities and assessment methods. d) 
Other information such as learning resources, classroom policies, grading system, etc. 
 
So how was OBE followed in the design of the syllabus?

The methods and assessment should be constructively aligned towards the attainment of 
the course outcomes. Course outcome refers to what learners are expected to know and be able to 
do at end of the course. Methods refer  to an activity or set of activities that will engage the 
student in achieving the course outcome. Assessment task refers to a tool that dete rmines how 
well the student has met the course outcome.

ILIGAN INSTITUTE OF TECHNOLOGY 
 
FORM 14 & 15 
 
Program Assessment and Evaluation and Continuous Quality Improvement 
for Graduate Programs of the College of Computer Studies 
 
 
1 Introduction 
The College of Computer Studies is committed to excellence in Computing Education by 
offering updated programs and application of computers. For that, our academic programs 
should always be aligned with the modern trends and standards of the discipline, and with the 
best recognized practices to produce reliable and successful professionals, as reflected in the 
vision and mission of the college. 
For the purpose of establishing a common ground of development, the college decided to 
formulate and adopt the following vision and mission: 
 
Vision A Center of Excellence in Information and Communication 
Technology manned by motivated and highly competent workforce 
who adheres to Institute policies; equipped with state -of-the-art 
infrastructure; founded on relevant instruction, strong culture of 
Research & Extension and enhanced resource -generating programs 
producing highly competitive graduates imbued with professional 
values. 
Mission Transform the deserving sector of the society into highly competitive 
graduates who will respond to the needs of the Local and International 
industries for ICT professionals. 
 
Hence, we have established a continuous improvement process aimed at evaluating the 
performance of the CCS programs to help us design and implement improvement actions 
needed to always stay aligned with this commitment. Moreover, in both academic programs, 
the continuous improvement process is perhaps more crucial because of the following reasons: 
• The computing discipline and areas of application are rapidly and continuously evolving, 
not only in deriving new knowledge but also in their impact in modern society. 
• We must always guarantee to our constituencies that our alumni are capable of fulfilling 
their professional duties in a satisfactory and reliable manner, and that they will be able 
to evolve in knowledge as the discipline evolves to be able to continue their contribution 
to the growth of our society through the practice of the profession. 
In summary, the continuous improvement process is designe d to guarantee that our programs 
enable our students to acquire a set of fundamental skills that every professional in the 
discipline must have. Those skills are captured in the student outcomes (SOs) of each 
program. The student outcomes are found in respective programs curriculum documentation. 
 
Checklist 14 & 15 

--- PAGE 54 ---
 
48 
 
 
2 College Quality Assurance Management Committee (CQuaM) 
To lead this continuous improvement effort, the College of Computer Studies has created the 
College Quality Assurance Management Committee (CQuaM) for the regular assessment and 
evaluation, and continuous quality improvement, composed of: 
1. College Dean 
2. Department Chairpersons 
3. Graduate Coordinator 
4. Department Representative of Graduate Programs 
5. Assessment Coordinator (Curriculum College Representative) 
6. Administrative Assistant 
 
Since the graduate programs are currently newly revised program, we started designing and 
implementing an assessment plan from the ground up. The plan itself should also be subject to 
continuous revision and improv ement. In the following sections, we detail our continuous 
assessment and improvement plan to guarantee attainment of SOs and how we have used it so 
far to implement specific improvement actions in specific courses. 
3 The Assessment Process 
The curricula in our programs are the component that is responsible for the most in fulfilling 
the student outcomes of the program, our plan concentrates on the continuous improvement 
of all our courses, especially the core and professional courses that are managed by ea ch 
department (department chairpersons) and program coordinators (each program). The plan is 
a two-component plan, as follows: 
 
3.1 Assessment Strategy Components 
Component 1. Assessment, evaluation, and continuous improvement of core and 
specialization courses in the program that are administered by each department, but not 
including the thesis courses (e.g., ending in 298 and 299 courses). 
The main goal of this component is to evaluate how each course is performing with 
regards to the specific goals defined for the course. Each course includes specific student 
learning outcome referred to as Course Learning Outcome (CLOs), which are specific to 
the topics that the course covers. The learning outcomes define important knowledge, 
skills, and disposition  that the student will attain after successfully passing the course. 
At the same time, those skills are important for the attainment of one or more SO. Since 
each CLO impacts at least one SO, this assessment will also identify how courses 
perform in terms of the SOs for which they are expected to contribute specific and 
relevant skills. All CLOs for our courses, as well as their mappings to SOs, have been 
incorporated into each official course syllabus. 
 
 
 

--- PAGE 55 ---
 
49 
 
Component 2. Assessment and evaluation of the thesis courses. 
We expect that our students are able to show attainment of the program SOs when they 
work in their corresponding thesis. Therefore, the thesis course will play a crucial role in 
the continuous improvement process of the whole program. From the results obtained on 
that course, we will be able to decide further improvement actions that might be required 
throughout the program. 
In the following subsections, we describe both components in detail. 
3.2 Direct Assessment, Evaluation, and Improvement of Professional Courses 
As previously described, each course has its own list of Course Learning Outcomes (CLOs), 
which represent the fundamental skills that the course will enable students to acquire, based 
on the particular topics and activities of the course. In addition, each CLO is mapped to one or 
more SOs that are directly impacted by the skills associated with the CLO. That mapping will 
allow us to assess not only how the course is performing in terms of its own CLOs, but also 
how it is performing with regard to the SOs that are being impacted. Hence, in the process of 
evaluating a course and determine improvement actions, the CLOs of the course are used as 
performance indicators. 
This approach simplifies the work required to prepare specific assessment  activities that are 
aligned to specific CLOs, and through which the performance of the course can be measured. 
It also helps us maintain uniformity in a given course when multiple faculty member teaches 
different sections, since the CLOs provide a framewo rk to build common educational 
activities. The assessment data for all courses are evaluated by the DQuaM Committee first to 
propose actions to the faculty of each program to improve the performance of the course. This 
will also be reported to members of the College Quality Management Committee.  
The table below shows the case of one particular core course in MS Computer Applications 
Program, MCA203 Systems Development With Emerging Technologies. It shows each of the 
CLOs for this course and the SOs of the program that each CLOs impacts. The expectation is 
that by attaining a particular CLO in the course, the student will be acquiring particular skills 
that contribute to one or more SOs as specified by the table. 
 
Upon successful completion of the course, the student will be able to: SO 
Understand the role of computer applications ITE in organization  4 
Apply ITE standard tools in managing ITE resources and related technologies 2 
Design and development of system prototype driven with emerging 
technologies 
6 
 
When a course is under assessment, it is expected that the professors in charge define how the 
CLOs of the course will be measured and evaluated. That effort will be done in conjunction 
with the DQuaM and reported later to CQuaM. If there are several professors in charge of 
multiple sections of the course, it is expected that they coordinate this effort, so that all sections 
use the same assessment instruments. Those instruments are activities that are to be graded and 

--- PAGE 56 ---
 
50 
 
are part of the work used in the course to determine the final grade of the students. For example, 
they could be: 
1. Quizzes 
2. Specific exercises in exams 
3. Home works 
4. Projects 
5. Laboratory work 
6. Written reports  
7. Oral presentations 
8. etc. 
 
Each of these instruments is targeted to measure a particular CLO, denoted as CLOx, as well 
as the SOs that the CLO impacts. This is a measure of how the students that passed the course 
performed on each CLO based on the selected graded work. This assessme nt process will 
include at least one activity for each CLO. All the scores for each student are normalized to 
100 as the maximum possible value on each such activity. Then, if there are m activities 
intended to measure a given CLOx, the maximum possible score that a student can get for that 
CLO would be 100m. The percentage corresponding to the total score reached by the student 
on those graded activities measuring CLOx is used to measure the level reached by the 
particular student on CLOx. Notice that this value is equal to the average of those m values that 
the student gets. The following illustrates this using some notation and formulas. 
If Si is the score that the student gets in the ith activity intended to measure CLOx, then the total 
score of that particular student would be 
m 
SCLOx =∑ Si 
i=1 
Based on this, the performance that the particular student exhibits for CLOx would be 
determined as

 × 100 = x 
 100m m 
For a student to be considered ”in good standing for CLOx”, the final value needs to be ≥ 70, 
or simply 
SCLO 
x ≥ 70 
m 
This approach is also applied to SOs in a course to measure performance of the course in terms 
of the SOs that it contributes to. This will be described in more detail in a coming subsection. 
  


--- PAGE 57 ---
 
51 
 
3.2.1 Performance of a Particular CLO on the Course 
To obtain a final measure of the performance of a CLOx in the course, we use the following 
metric: the percentage of passing students that end in good standing with respect to CLOx. 
3.2.2 Mapping CLOs Performance to the CS Program SOs 
Curriculum maps are those for which the course is expected to contribute with the particular 
set of knowledge and skills that the students would have acquired by attaining its CLOs. The 
table in Appendix B details an example of mapping of the CLOs to the student outcomes. The 
letter in each cell indicates the level of support that a course will contribute (or impact) to the 
achievement of a particular SO. 
It should also be noted that the activities used to measure the performance of CLOs are als o 
used to measure performance of SOs, and that is done following the same approach. A student 
passing the course will be in good standing with respect to a particular SO j (we shall denote 
as SOj) if the student score at least 70% in the corresponding graded activities. To finally 
measure the performance of a particular SOj in the course, we use the following metric: the 
percentage of passing students that are in good standing with respect to SOj. 
3.3 Continuous Assessment and Improvement Process of Professional Courses 
In this subsection, we discuss the details of the cyclical process established for the continuous 
assessment and improvement process for core and specialization courses. We also discuss the 
data collection mechanism and the assessment of the thesis courses. 
3.3.1 Details of the Assessment Cycle 
In order to guarantee timely and frequent assessment and continuous improvement of all the 
core and specialization courses, while minimizing the additional workload imposed on our 
faculty, we have divided our courses into two groups according to how each course is being 
planned to be offered in the program’s curricula. Each semester we focus our improvement 
efforts on only one of these groups, following a round-robin approach. Thus, all courses must 
have been assessed every two years. And during each 2-year cycle, we will have the chance to 
implement proposed improvement actions for every core or specialization course on each 
offering of the course as part of the continuous improvement process. Courses that are offered 
every semester will have two semesters to implement those actions before the next time that it 
is under direct assessment as per our assessment plan. Those that are offered every o ther 
semester, will have at least two semesters to implement those actions or as soon as it is agreed 
to implement the needed improvement. 
Based on our current programs’ curricula, each of the core and specialization courses needs to 
be offered at least as illustrated in Appendix C, in the period of two (2) years.  
Based on the distribution of courses by semester (Appendix C), we divided the core and 
professional courses into two groups. Each group will correspond to a set of 2 to 4 courses that 
are schedule to be offered on the same semester. The course groups, as well as the semesters 
in which they are planned to be under assessment are shown in table below. 
 
 

--- PAGE 58 ---
 
52 
 
Group Schedule Courses 
1 Sem 1 One or two core courses 
2 Sem 2 One or two specialization courses 
 
In addition to those two groups, the thesis courses of the program are going to be under 
assessment on every semester that they are offered (We say more about the assessment of the 
thesis courses later on.). 
On a particular semester, our effort will concentrate in the group of courses that corresponds 
to that semester as per the plan. Assessment data and results will be collected by professors 
offering the particular courses. That data will be evaluated by the DQuaM in collaboration  
with CQuaM, in conjunction with the professors teaching the course. From that analysis, 
decisions are made for necessary improvement actions in each particular course. Those actions 
will be implemented on the successive semesters that the course is offered. The whole faculty 
will be informed of results and consulted on those actions that may require departmental or 
college approval. 
3.3.2 Course Assessment and Improvement Cycle 
Figure 1 summarizes the general assessment and improvement cycle of courses per semester. 
The assessment process on each course of the group of courses being under assessment on a 
particular semester is as follows: 
1. Beginning of the semester: 
DQuaM meets with all faculty members teaching the course to or the department will  
decide for courses under assessment: 
(a) Review course learning outcomes 
(b) Select the student activities and rubrics to be used to assess each CLO 
(c) Develop a data collection plan using standardized tools such as forms and 
spreadsheets whenever possible 
2. Throughout the semester: 
(a) Faculty members carry out assessment activities and collect data 

--- PAGE 59 ---
 
53 
 
 
Figure 1: Assessment and Improvement Cycle 
 
(b) The DQuaM (or identified faculty members) will aid faculty members to tabulate 
data and store documents 
3. End of semester: 
(a) Each faculty member submits a FCAR form capturing their overall assessment on 
course learning outcome performance, their impressions about the course based on 
their experience, and suggestions for improving the course 
(b) Faculty members submit tabulated CLO assessment data and student work samples 
that support those results 
(c) CQuaM archives all submitted data to facilitate future access by accreditation 
agencies 
(d) CQuaM analyzes all the information submitted by faculty members 
4. Beginning of next semester: 
(a) CQuaM meets with faculty members to device specific CLO improvement actions 
to be implemented for this course during the successive semesters 
(b) All department through its program coordinator will make sure that those 
improvement actions are carried out even  if new faculty members are assigned to 
the course 
5. Throughout next three semesters: 
(a) Faculty members incorporate the improvement actions to their courses 
(b) The Department and College management will stay in close watch with the offering 
of the course to suppo rt the implementation of the approved actions and to make 
corrections as needed 
  


--- PAGE 60 ---
 
54 
 
In essence, individual course assessment is fundamentally achieved by measuring the 
performance of the students passing the course in all of the CLOs of the course. Using that 
data, and the pre-established mapping from CLOs of the course to SOs, we also get the results 
measure their performance in terms of the SOs that the course is expected to contribute to. The 
CQuaM, in conjunction with the faculty teaching the course, will analyze possible reasons for 
those one or two CLOs, as well as SOs, that exhibit lowest performance values. Then, an 
improvement plan, which may include modifying existing course activities or incorporating 
new ones, is developed to improve student performance on those weakest outcomes. 
3.3.3 Departmental/College Faculty Retreats for Assessment and Continuous 
Improvement 
The departments and college are committed to organize a faculty retreat at least once per year. 
The retreat will include in the  agenda the following important tasks regarding the continuous 
improvement process: 
1. Report status on faculty participation in the assessment process for the two groups of 
courses that were under direct assessment in the two preceding semesters. 
2. Report assessment data that has been collected during the previous two semesters. 
3. Report results of the preliminary evaluation based on collected assessment data that must 
have been done by DQuaM or CQuaM. 
4. Discuss and approve continuous improvement actions recommende d by DQuaM and 
CQuaM. 
5. Other new issues. 
Some urgent issues may be presented and discussed in regular college meetings too. For that, 
every departmental or college meeting will have in the agenda an open item to discuss relevant 
issues regarding the continuous improvement process. 
3.3.4 Data Collection Mechanism 
The DQuaM or CQuaM are aware that the participation of the faculty is crucial in this 
assessment process. And also, that the more the additional work the process imposes on that 
faculty the less encouraged will they be to effectively provide their assessment part. With that 
in mind, the CquaM or DQuaM will utilize Google Sheet forms that the faculty can use to 
facilitate the submission of data for the courses under assessment. From that form, once the  
data is submitted, it automatically generates the results for each of the CLOs of the course 
based on what was previously discussed. Those forms will also show how each SO being 
impacted by the course is performing based on the data that is provided. 
A specific form will be created for each course to fill in student’s data from the course 
activities. When the course is under assessment, that form is shared with the faculty teaching 
the course, so that they can enter the data once the semester is finished . We will see concrete 
cases of the results generated using these forms when we discuss specific results in a later 
section of this report. The complete form will be available for review, including those with 
specific data for courses that have already gone through direct assessment, during the days of 
the site visit or as requested by the program evaluators. 

--- PAGE 61 ---
 
55 
 
4 The Faculty Course Assessment Report (FCAR) 
For each course, the DQuaM or CquaM will create a template corresponding to the Faculty 
Course Assessment Report (FCAR). That template allows the faculty member in-charge of the 
course to submit a summary of the results of their course assessment. That report is later used 
to determine improvement actions as per the previous discussion. The FCAR provides a 
summary of both CLO and SO attainment in the course. It is expected that only one FCAR is 
submitted summarizing the results in all sections of the course; either if it is a single or multiple 
faculty members handling the course during the semester. The even tual evaluation and 
decisions about improvement based on that assessment will be done by the DQuaM in 
conjunction with CQuaM. 
At this moment the FCAR is still a working document, and therefore we should expect 
modifications in the near future. Currently, the form includes three essential parts. These are: 
1. Coverage of Topics in the Course 
This is a measure of the coverage level of the particular topic in the semester being 
reported, based on what is expected at the particular course. The purpose of this data is 
to determine what topics are constantly being left out and make decisions based on that 
knowledge. 
2. Assessment of Course Learning Outcomes using Graded Work 
This corresponds to the assessment of CLOs as was previously described. For a course  
having n CLOs, that table would be as follows:

5 Assessment of Thesis Courses 
Courses in our programs are under constant assessment in order to measure how each course 
needs to be improved for successfully fulfilling the role that particular courses have in the 
whole academic program. As previously explained, we measure how the stud ents passing the 
course did with respects to the CLOs of the particular course. That is mapped to the SOs, based 
on the ones that each CLO impacts. Moreover, the final determination as to whether our 
graduating students are reaching the needed skills to at tain the student outcomes is based on 
how they perform in their thesis courses. Hence the thesis course will be under assessment on 
each occasion that it is offered. In such offering, we measure how students perform in each 
student outcome. 
In the case of the thesis courses, there are no CLOs per se. Instead, we measure how the 
students perform in each of the SOs based on the following activities: 
1. Written project proposal 
2. Written project reports 
3. Oral project presentations 
4. Demonstrations of the project artifacts (e.g., mobile app, software tool, web app) 
For each such activity, the course instructors or thesis supervisors have an evaluation 
instrument that scores technical content, presentation skills, innovation, social/ethical issues, 
and softw are development techniques and tools used to implement the solution. This 
instrument maps key activities directly to the program SOs. Scores from all activities 
performed during the semester are accumulated, averaged, and mapped to the SOs. Then, these 
are converted to a percentage score. We currently use 70% as the minimum attainment score 
that indicates that student performance is adequate for a given SO. A score lower than this 
starts a process to look deeper into possible actions that must be taken to improve the program. 
6 Expected Level of Attainment of Student Outcomes 
Our goal from the assessment of a particular course is to identify which specific SOs that the 
course impacts are shown to have below average performance. Therefore, we have not 
established any additional threshold to establish if performance on a student outcome is 
adequate or not for a particular course. Our goal is to always find some area for improvement, 
even if they are minor, by focusing on those CLOs and SOs that show the lowest performance 
in the course. If several CLOs or SOs are very low in a particular course, we try to identify 
which are the most relevant CLOs to improve. We expect that if we improve in one particular 
CLO it might have a positive impact in other CLOs as well.

Eligibility criteria 
The College of Computer Studies is recognized as a CHED Center of Development in Information 
Technology Education. At present, CCS offers a total of 7 academic programs, i.e., with 4 at the 
undergraduate-level and 3 at the graduate-level, hosted in different Departments as highlighted in 
the table below. All the undergraduate program offerings of the College are also AACCUP Level 
2 re-accredited. 
  
Department Undergraduate program offering Graduate program offering 
Computer Applications BS Computer Applications MS Computer Applications 
Computer Science BS Computer Science MS Computer Science 
Information 
Technology 
BS Information Systems 
BS Information Technology 
MS Information Technology 
  
Number of qualified faculty 
The Computer Applications program hosts 8 core faculty with varied specializations. Three of the 
core faculty members hold a Doctoral degree  or with Doctoral units . The rest of the faculty 
members are equipped with Masteral degree. There are 3 other faculty members with Doctoral 
degrees from the other programs within the College.

FORM 18 
 
LIST OF SCHOOL ADMINISTRATORS AND NEEDED QUALIFICATIONS AND 
REQUIREMENTS AND FUNCTIONS 
 
 
MASTER OF SCIENCE IN COMPUTER APPLICATIONS 
 
 
Qualifications of Administrators 
 
The Master of Science in Computer Applications is managed by the Director of the School 
of Graduate Studies and the Dean of the College of Computer Studies through the CCS Graduate 
Studies Coordinator. 
 
As mentioned in CMO 15 s. 2019, the a bove administrators shall respectively be holders 
of a doctoral degree in the discipline or allied fields and have published works in refereed journals 
in their respective disciplines.     
 
Functions of Administrators 
 
 
Director of the Center for Advanced Education and Lifelong Learning (CAELL) 
 
As director of the Center for Advanced Education and Lifelong Learning (CAELL), the following 
functions shall be discharged: 
 
1.  Coordinate with all the colleges in planning, administrating, monitoring, evalu ating, and 
enhancing graduate programs of the Institute; 
2. Ensure the policies concerning graduate programs are implemented and the procedures 
followed; 
3. Coordinate with Institutions and other Government Agencies in matters relating to graduate 
programs;  
4. Provide support and assistance in the development and approval of new programs;  
5. Ensure new programs proposed by each department of the Institute are consistent with 
Commission on Higher Education (CHED) Policies and Procedures, and any other relevant 
professional, regulatory, or statutory bodies; 
6. Put in place quality assurance mechanisms, and ensure faculty and staff perform their 
duties competently; 
7. Secure the accreditation and recognition of graduate programs; 
8. Ensure that the career development plans of the faculty and staff of the graduate programs 
are implemented; and  
9. Recommend to the Vice Chancellor for Academic Affairs the hiring of Graduate Teaching 
Assistants (GTAs). 
 
 
The designated director is entitled to twelve (12) units administrative load and accumulated leave 
credits during the term of the designation or proportionate to the period of service rendered (subject 
to the approval of the DBM), to representation and transportation allowance (RATA).  Please see 
Appendices for the supporting documents of the current Director, Dr. Cecilia B. Tangian. 
 
 
  
Checklist 18 

--- PAGE 68 ---
 
62 
 
College Dean  
 
As Dean of the college, the following functions shall be discharged, in addition to those 
discussed in the University Code: 
 
1.  Assume responsibility in the administration of the college; 
2. Initiate, prepare, review, and present plans to higher authorities for the development of    the  
college, and implement approved programs;  
3. Exercise supervision and control over the faculty members and non-academic personnel of 
the college;  
4. Supervise the evaluation of performance of the faculty members and non -academic 
personnel of the college;  
5.  Recommend allocation of available funds, in accordance with approved plans; 
6. Keep management informed regarding major developments in the college, and justify the 
college budget proposal;  
7. Supervise the admission and classification of students;  
8. Make recommendations on hiring, promotion, and granting of tenure of personnel in the 
college;  
9. Initiate the development of learning resources, extension and research programs in the 
college;  
10. Perform other functions as may be required by higher authority.  
 
The designated Dean is entitled to  a 12-unit equivalent teaching load and to leave c redits and 
shall serve for a period of three years.  Please see Appendices for the supporting documents of the 
current Dean, Dr. Rabby Q. Lavilles. 
 
 
Graduate Program Coordinator  
 
The designated Graduate Program Coordinator of the College of Computer Studies shall 
discharge the following functions, in addition to those defined in the University Code:  
 
1. Perform administrative duties and assist the Dean in the implementation of gradua te 
programs;  
2. Recommend to the Dean, in coordination with the Department Chairperson, schedule of 
course offerings and teaching load of faculty members and lecturers;  
3. Review and refine the general implementing rules and regulations of graduate programs;  
4. Screen the admission of an applicant, by examining submitted credentials as to whether or 
not the general requirements of the School are fully satisfied;  
5. Recommend the application for admission of the student for approval by higher authorities;  
6. Recommend jointly with the other Program Coordinators/Adviser the suspension/ 
dismissal of a student from further work in the program if the student’s performance is 
found consistently unsatisfactory;  
7. Recommend a research adviser and members of the advisory / oral examination panel;  
8. Recommend jointly with other Program Coordinators/Adviser approval of formal request 
for transfer of credits;  
9. Identify, in consultation with the Adviser, the academic status of a student;  
10. Recommend admission to candidacy for a master’s/doctorate degree;  
11. Sit in special project/thesis/dissertation oral examinations; 
12. Perform such other duties as may be assigned by the Dean. 
 
 The designated Graduate Program Coordinator shall be entitled to six units administrative load 
and leave credits , in accordance with BOR Res No. 114, s.2007. The Graduate Program 
Coordinator shall serve for one year. Please see Appendix C for the supporting documents of the 
current Graduate Program Coordinator: Erik Louwe R. Sala, MSIT.

Mindanao State University 
ILIGAN INSTITUTE OF TECHNOLOGY 
FORM 21 
STUDENT ENROLMENT 
 MASTER OF SCIENCE IN COMPUTER APPLICATIONS 
 
The Master of Science in Computer Applications (MSCA) which was approved through 
BOR No. 7 Series of 2004 , hosted by the College of Computer Studies (CCS) and the 
Center for Advanced Education and Lifelong Learning (CAELL).  The projected clientele 
of the proposed MSCA program are expected to come from the ICT companies and 
academe, to include fresh graduates .  The following HEIs in Visayas and Mindanao, 
among others,  are potential sources of Master of Science in Computer Applications  
students: 
  
   MSU-IIT 
   Leyte Normal University 
   Southwestern University 
   Liceo de Cagayan University 
   Xavier University 
 
 
Other prospect students will be from the government agencies, the non-state organizations, 
private organizations.  Herein is an enumeration of these organizations. 
  
  Local Government Units (LGUs) 
  National Government Agencies 
  Non-Government Organizations 
  Private Organizations 
  Private Individuals who wish to pursue Master of Science in Computer 
Applications

RESOURCES FOR RESEARCH WORKS INCLUDING ELECTRONIC DATABASE

Electronic databases of both international and national reputable journals are accessible to the 
students through internet. The College of Computer Studies (CCS), College of Science and 
Mathematics (CSM), College of Business Administration and Accountancy (CBAA) provide 
internet connection readily available for student use.  
 
The Library also has an On-line Public Catalog (OPAC). The On-line Public Access Catalog is an 
update of the traditional catalog. Bibliographic records of library collection are viewed in the 
OPAC through the use of a computer terminal. One can search a keyword or subject, author, title, 
or a combination of author and title, keyword and title, etc.  
 
Below is the list of resources that students can access to enhance their research work.

around issues of teaching and learning;

• Foster and recognize excellence and innovation in 
teaching and learning;

opportunities for continuous professional development for 
academics; 
• Facilitate sustainable change and teach ing and learning 
innovations in partnership with academics and academic 
units;

and socially-driven curriculum and program;

instructional and learning materials;

The Center for Information and Communication Technology 
(CICT) formerly named the Information and Communications 
Technology Center (ICTC) was created to coordinate and focus 
IT activities in order to minimize costs as well as maximize 
benefits from the synergy of the various program components that 
would significantly contribute to a successful IT E related  
programs, among others.  
 
The ICTC was formally established through Special Order 511 
IIT Series of 2002, Implementing Rules and Regulations for BOR 
Resolution Number 392 Series of 2002 Authorizing the 
Operationalization of the Information and Communication 
Technology Center (ICTC) on October 3, 2002.

Educating the Net generation : [website]. 
Website containing project materials, presentations and 
resources relating to "Educating the Net generation" project. 
Educating the Net Generation is a collaborative project 
involving the University of Melbourne, the University of 
Wollongong, and Charles Sturt University. The project, funded 
by the Australian Learning and Teaching Council, began in June 
2006. It involved an investigation into students' and teachers' 
use of new technologies and the develo pment of eight case 
studies in which emerging technologies were implemented in 
learning settings across the three participating universities. The 
aims of the project are to to develop and implement 
pedagogically sound, technology-based tools to enhance student 
learning in local learning environments.

Imagining the Internet : a history and forecast 
A multi -section resource containing thousands of pages. It 
exposes future possibilities while simultaneously providing a 
look back at the past. This resource includes special explanatory 
sections on the future and past development of communications 
networks. It includes videos and audio recordings that reveal the 
ideas of cutting -edge thinkers such as Douglas Engelbart and 
Vernor Vinge as they discuss the future. It has informative areas 
targeted to benefit children under 12 (KidZone), and to help 
teachers of elementary, middle school, high school and college 
students use the site to educate .The site shares a look back at 
what people were saying in the 1990s during the "awe" stage of 
the development of the internet, and it records a legacy of 
predictive statements as they are made. 
https://www.elon.edu/predict
ions/ 
InCites. Essential science indicators 
InCites: Essential science indicators can determine the 
influential individuals, institutions, papers, publications, and 
countries in a field of study--as well as emerging research areas 
that could impact your work. Conduct ongoing, quantitative 
analyses of research performance and trends in science. Rank 
top scientists,institutions, countries, and journals in 22 specific 
fields of research in science performa nce statistics and science 
trends based on journal article publication counts and citation 
data. Essential Science Indicators is the ideal resource for

Extension Program 
 
 
 
MSU-IIT VISION 
 
A university committed to the holistic development of the individual and society. 
 
 
MSU-IIT MISSION 
 
To provide quality education for the development of Mindanao and the country through relevant 
programs in instruction, research and community engagement. 
 
 
MSU-IIT OBJECTIVES 
 
● To develop and implement training programs geared to meet the technical and skilled 
manpower requirements of the specific type, magnitude, and level of competence needed 
by existing and projected industries in Iligan City and its environs; 
 
● To initiate and undertake projects and studies which bear on the manpower  needs, 
industrial growth of Iligan, and other development projects including those needed by 
specific industries; 
 
● To organize and implement, as needed by the community, academic programs for the 
development of the technical and professional manpower that will enhance and support the 
industrial growth of Iligan within the economic and social development plan for Mindanao. 
 
    
MSU-IIT Core Values 
 
To realize our vision, mission, and goals, we dedicate ourselves to the following code of 
conduct: 
 
It is in this Institute framework that MSU -IIT draws out its Institutionalized WE CARE Extension 
Program per BOR# 298 s. 2014 with its subsequent short and long term extension engagements with 
its clientele and partners.  
Checklist 26 

--- PAGE 108 ---
 
102 
 
 
 
WE CARE Program  
Institutionalized Extension Program per BOR# 298 s. 2014 
 
W - Wellness/Health Sanitation C - Capability Building 
E - Education/Literacy  A - Alternative Livelihood 
     R - Rehabilitation of the Environment 
     E - Entrepreneurial Development 
 
The formulation of the Institute’s Extension Agenda was based on several key factors: mission and 
vision of the Institute; track record of faculty in specific areas in research; strength of graduate faculty; 
international refereed publications; recognized cent ers of excellence; convergence of research and 
development agenda of government line agencies (DOST, CHED, DOH, DA, NEDA, etc.); the United 
Nations’ millennium development goals (MDG); regional trends; and community needs. Research and 
Development endeavor s that are multi -disciplinary, collaborative and aligned with the identified 
agenda shall be given priority 
 
a. COMMUNITY EMPOWERMENT: Adopt-a-barangay/community program; Village modeling; 
Livelihood and entrepreneurial skills development; Health and sanit ation, Community organizing; 
Leadership; Feeding program; Gender and development (GAD); 
 
b. ENVIRONMENT & SUSTAINABLE DEVELOPMENT : Natural resources management; 
Community forest management; Biodiversity; Climate change; Disaster risk reduction and 
management; Sustainable agriculture; Al Khalifa (Islamic concept of environmental management); 
Maximizing customer skills; Public service excellence program; Public service ethics and 
accountability; Promoting anti-corruption efforts; Preparation of executive-legislative agenda, Socio-
economic profile, Comprehensive land use plan and forest land use plan; Sustainable integrated area 
development. 
 
c. PEACE & DEVELOPMENT: Barangay justice for peace; Interfaith dialogue; Conflict resolution; 
Islamic forum. 
 
d. TECHNOL OGYTRANSFER: Technology diffusion or adoption; Technology business 
incubation facility; Tool packing facility. 
 
e. EDUCATIONAL ENRICHMENT:  Adopt-a-school; Accelerated teacher’s training; Functional 
literacy; Program for the physically-challenged. 
 
f. TECHNICAL ASSISTANCE: Investment promotion; Enterprise development. 
From the MSU -IIT Core Values down to its Institutionalized Extension Programs, the College of 
Computer Studies hosting the BS in Computer Applications has developed priority areas for its 
extension programs through the CCS 5Es Priorities to focus its extension programs. 
 
 
COLLEGE OF COMPUTER STUDIES 5Es Extension Priorities 
e - education | e – government | e - society / environment| e- innovation / business | e- health 
 
It is in this CCS 5Es Extension Priorities that the extension programs will be implemented as the 
relevant program in extension and community involvement to realize the Mission of MSU -IIT to 
contribute to the industrial and socio -economic development of Mi ndanao. The CCS 5Es Extension 
Priorities is the application of Information and communications technology to the areas of Mindanao’s 
education, government, society, environment, business and health. With these Extension Priorities, the 
Masters of Science in Computer Applications implements the integration of technology intervention 
to the different strata of Mindanao’s society and contribute to the Mission of MSU -IIT which is to 
contribute to the industrial and socio-economic development of Mindanao through relevant programs 
in extension and community involvement.

5. Safety health policies and standards for classrooms 
against COVID 19 
 
Available on the website of MSU-IIT is the COVID 19 activities and 
policies to transition to the “new normal” Activities and policies 
include:  
 
● Strict implementation of the safety protocols to prevent the 
transmission of the virus 
● Addressed the need for social distancing by setting up 
alternative work arrangement and virtualizing the 
administration as much as possible 
● Implementing alternative learning arrangements through 
Special Order No. 00835-IIT, S. 2020, MSU-IIT promulgated 
the guidelines for its innovative flexible teaching and learning 
(InFlex) delivery that will function as a way for teachers and 
students to have classes without being physically present. 
● With the decision to pursue innovative flexible teaching and 
learning (In Flex) delivery of lessons that would see the first 
semester of AY 2020-2021 opening in September 2, MSU-
IIT is employing its e-resources so students do not have to be 
physically present in the campus when they enrol in their 
classes starting July 1, 2020. 
● The enrolment period that will cover the whole month of July 
will utilize the MSU-IIT Intelligent Course Adviser 
(MICA)and the electronic School Management System 
(Esms) 
MICA and Esms are enrolment apps designed by the 
Institute’s Center for Information and Communication 
Technology (CICT) 
● The Center for Pedagogical Innovations (CPI) formerly know 
as the Center for eLearning (MICEL), in collaboration with 
the Center for Information and Communication Technology 
(CICT), provided a quick training to the employees to shift 
towards remote working or work from home.
